quora评论


﻿Question:

Computer Science Education: Which is more impressive in Silicon Valley, a Harvard CS grad or a CMU CS grad?
Given equal skill level -- my question stems from the fact that Harvard is much more prestigious than CMU as a whole, but the CS department at CMU is ranked much higher than Harvard's and is one of the "Big 4 CS schools".
Answer:
Coming from CMU I can vouch for the fact that just the brand name of CMU will get you interviews at most Bay Area firms, however impressive candidates are the ones with good experience and impressive skill sets that actually end up getting the job.

From my experience in the valley, recruiters care more about their skill sets and people from lesser known colleges have gotten interviews at many of the top companies here.

Just because you're from Harvard or CMU or Stanford doesn't mean you're impressive or that you have extraordinary abilities. And trust me there is no prestige in the valley, no one will care about your degree in a few years, they care about the work that you do or the skills that you have. That's it.
Answer:
It's obvious to me that CMU is more impressive in the CS field. Microsoft is not a Silicon Valley company, but I guess the culture is similar. We have many VPs and GMs and Distinguished Engineers that graduated from CMU. I have not met a Harvard CS grad in the company.

Actually if you go to Harvard or Yale and study CS, the first question people will ask is "Why?" I have a colleague who send his son to Yale CS. During lunch or coffee discussions, he has to constantly defend why Yale CS is better than UW's or UC Berkeley's. Aside from the Ivy League name, it is really hard to make sense.
Answer:
I've held a couple architect level positions where I would help make hiring decisions. I've never been so fortunate as to have had some many great candidates where I could be so picky about schools. I would only see the resumes that made it past HR, but if you had the relevant skills / experience, you would get at least a phone interview. School choice didn't matter to me, let alone which Ivy League it was.

It may be a little more relevant when you are straight out of college, but these days, I expect top performing grads to have independent projects and find reviewing those both worthy of my time and again more relevant then which school you went to.

Developers are hired much more based on skills then degrees. Your best choice of school is likely the one where you will make the most friends who can become part of your network later on in life and can help you get your second or third job. I don't think you can go wrong with either Harvard or CMU. You could do much worse and still have no problem getting a programming job.

For Silicon Valley, your best bet, by far, is Stanford just because it's in Silicon Valley and many people making hiring decisions will have come from it or know others who have and many recruit directly from it.
Written Nov 8


Question:

What differentiates the CMU CS graduate program from other top programs?
Answer:
If you are interested in functional programming and/or programming languages theory, CMU's POP group has much deeper faculty than the other top 4 schools.

Also, Pittsburgh is substantially cheaper than the Bay Area or Cambridge/Boston, so your stipend/fellowship will go a lot farther.
Answer:
CMU CS grads generally are very, very application focused when compared with Harvard or MIT. This is a trait shared with Stanford CS grad school as well.

It is this way for historical reasons, from the collaboration between ECE/RI and Stats departments on AI research from late 70s/early 80s onwards, and the huge presence of business process optimization folks at Heinz/Tepper. The fact that RAND corp, Google, Adobe, Disney, Microsoft and now Uber are pretty much on campus also helps the focus.


Question:

What are the best courses at CMU?
This is a follow-up question to What are the best courses at MIT?.
Also see What are the worst courses to take at CMU?
Answer:
15-857 Performance Modeling and Design of Computer Systems : I opted for this course around 2 weeks into the semester, cancelling one my other classes, after reading Jackson Davis's answer here which said take up any course taught by Mor Harchol-Balter. And I sincerely thank him for that. (Also, after registering for it, I saw the course webpage and noticed that it wasn't going to be offered again until Fall '14) Although this is the same course that he refers to as Queuing theory hell and it certainly takes a lot out of you, it is the best course I have taken till date and Mor is the best teacher I have learnt from. It was a fast-paced class but very nicely organized. We covered everything from her yet-to-release text book Performance Modeling and Design of Computer Systems: Queueing Theory in Action. Every other lecture, she used to pose to us, open problems from her research which I found exciting to try. (though I didn't get anywhere with them most of the times) The class was very taxing but for the sheer amount of new material that I learnt from this class, it was totally worth it.

36-705 (also 10-705) Intermediate Statistics : It was a class which covered many diverse topics rather than a few in too much depth. You can see the topics covered here. Apart from the content though, what made it special was Larry Wasserman. I found his cool, calm way of teaching quite unique and his well-made lecture notes also helped a lot. I don't remember a single question asked by a student to which he gave an unsatisfactory answer (and there were many of them, as you would expect in a class of around 70-80 students) In fact, very often, his answers were embellished with interesting examples from his own research and experience that helped highlight the important concepts taught in the course.

I hope I can add more to this list in the future
Answer:
15-251 Great Theoretical Ideas in Computer Science, with Luis Von Ahn, taught me to love problem solving and think about computer science in a more organized and mathematical way
15-210 Parallel and Sequential Data Structures and Algorithms, with Guy Blelloch, gives a pretty thorough and elegant account of most key data structures and algorithms. Professor Blelloch (and other course staff) put together a really great set of lecture notes that are precise and clear. The homework for the course is atypical of programming-based algorithms courses in that when you're presented with a problem, you're expected to first solve it and only then put together the actual code, rather than having the algorithm handed to you.
15-312 Principles of Programming Languages with Bob Harper shaped the way I think about programming more than anything else at Carnegie Mellon. Over the semester and in his textbook, Harper develops elegant and extremely precise mathematical frameworks based on type theory to formalize various programming paradigms, from simple arithmetic to object-oriented programming, laziness, and various models for concurrent programs. Proofs and programs both take on a new meaning within 312's framework.
15-359 Probability and Computing with Mor Harchol-Balter (and whoever else is teaching it with her—hopefully Klaus Sutner) was a great introduction to probability theory. Homeworks are fairly difficult and often similar to the familiar 251 puzzle-style problems.
15-411 Compiler Design was awesome. You write an immense amount of code with a fairly large amount of freedom. Each lab involves adding new features to each part of your compiler, so you're forced to focus on writing maintainable code with smart abstractions. I recommend trying a new programming language to work with rather than Standard ML. (Let me know if you pick Scala so I can give you some advice….)

Special Mentions:
33-104 Experimental Physics was one of the easiest classes I've taken at CMU
Information Warfare might not be around anymore, but if they bring it back how can you say no to 12 units of A for virtually no work?
The Beatles with Schultz was really easy and quite fun.
When Hayes taught 85-211 Cognitive Psychology (Spring 2011) the exams were open book and open notes. This made it quite easy to do well in the class.
Answer:
15-411 Compiler Design and 15-410 Operating Systems are great project courses with tons of useful content. 15-312 Fundamentals of Programming Languages changed the way I think about code and proofs more than any other course. These three have been invaluable in real-world programming.

54-191 Acting for Non-Majors is also a great course, especially if you're afraid to take it.
Answer:
Here to offer the non-technical opinion! I don't know the course numbers because in CFA most classes are referred to by name (or a shortened nickname).

Basic Interaction Design with John Zimmerman (or Jodi Forlizzi). This was probably one of the best classes I took in school. It changed my perspective on what interaction design was (or what I thought it was) and John is the kind of professor that will challenge you, understand your strengths and weakness as an individual student (not just an anonymous member of the class) and push you to be better. He is very critical, so taking a class with him is not for the type who can't separate their personal feelings from their work. Do not take this class with an HCI or IxD graduate student (they teach it too for the same amount of credit). It's a completely difference experience. (Referred to as "B.I.D.")

Time, Motion, & Communication with Dan Boyarski. The studios can run a little long and it's at 8:30 in the morning, but Dan is extremely welcome to non-majors and he made it a point to show how time and motion are applicable to all fields. The projects are fun and kind of therapeutic (albeit tedious) to work on. (Referred to as "Time")

Information & Interaction with Stacie Rohrbach. I didn't actually take this course, but instead I did an independent study with Stacie on the same topic. If you want to do anything related to information design, Stacie is the go-to person.

Mapping & Diagramming with Karen Moyer. Karen Moyer is like... an institution in the school of design. She's crazy and she told Paul Rand to f— off when she was a student, which instantly makes her a bad ass. In M&D you spend half the semester doing a series of short projects and then the second half working on one big information design piece of your choice. There are also a lot of show and tell classes where students just show cool visualizations. (Referred to as "Mapping")

Digital Imaging / Digital Color Output with Dylan Vitone. If you like photography, you should take a class with Dylan. I TA'd Digital Imaging with him, so I'm kind of bias... but if you're new to Photoshop, it's a good project-based class to take with a strong learning curve in the program. Dylan knows his stuff and he's one of the few professors that actually speaks the language of young people. He's also a practicing photographer who does some really cool work. (Referred to as "Dylan's Class")

Color & Communication with Kristen Hughes and Mark Mentzer. Frankly, just take any class with Mark Mentzer. That man is a national treasure and I feel like he teaches fewer classes every year. Color is very competitive class to get into, but I believe they opened up a non-major version as of this year. Which is probably for the best because my only qualm with that class was that we spent half the time going over basic design principles and there was a clear imbalance of expectations between majors and non-majors. Still, fun topic. (Referred to as "Color")

History of Books with my girl, Mary Kay. I can't remember if that was the exact course title, but basically... I took this half-semester night class to fulfill a "design topic" requirement in my major. I ended up actually enjoying the course. Yes, it's at night and it's three hours long (but more often than not we got out earlier), but the workload is pretty light (for CMU standards) and you get to spend one night a week randomly perusing books in the library's rare book room. The highlight of the course, for me, was stumbling upon a handwritten note by Charles Darwin in a first edition copy of On The Origin of Species.

Also have to add, either Letterpress & Bookbinding or Letterpress in the Digital World with Joe Dicey and Matt Griffin, respectively. While I have a few issues with both classes, I think the subject as a whole in fascinating and if you're in a very tech-heavy major like design or computer science, doing something tangible with your hands for six hours a week is a very relaxing experience.
Answer:
As an ECE major in the late 90's, I'll have to say 18-347 Computer Architecture and 18-747 Superscalar Processor Design taught by Prof. John Paul Shen was two of my favorite classes.

15-410 Operating Systems was not fun at the time - it totally kicked my butt. But skills learned there has proved to be invaluable for my career in writing embedded firmware.

I also have good memories of 15-461 Computer Graphics.

21-127 Modern Mathematics really introduced me to what the real mathematics was about.

All of these courses (and many others that I have not listed) challenges you to the core and really teaches you how to think. The more you put into it the more you will get out of them.
Answer:

Answer:
15-359 Probability and Computing, with Ryan O'Donnell. His lectures are excellent, he's very smart, and has made his notes available as a textbook online.

15-410 Operating Systems. The professor, Dave Eckhardt, is great, and the course requires you to write an operating system from scratch. You learn how to make design tradeoffs and to sustain programming effort over a months-long assignment.

21-484 Graph Theory with Oleg Pikhurko. He explains things well and covers nontrivial theorems usually taught in graduate courses.

Question:

What's the difference between CMU IS and CMU SCS for undergrad?
Why are they separated?
Can I still take classes in systems/information security if I get into the School of Computer Science?
Answer:
When I first came to CMU, I was torn between the CS and IS programs. I ended up pursuing IS and Human-Computer Interaction and am now a senior in the program. Here's my take on the two programs.


An "information system" is basically a combination of technology, people, and processes to meet some organizational goal. The IS program is all about designing and building these "information systems", not just technologies themselves, to meet the needs of organizations and businesses.

In practical terms, you learn to study how an organization works, identify where their weaknesses are, and how to build software to support their needs.

There are only five core IS classes and they tend to be pretty easy. You'll spend a lot of time reading and analyzing case studies about system failures (for example, the rollout of HR software at a huge company), trying to determine exactly how it failed along the dimensions of "people, process, and technology." The IS capstone course allows you to work with a real client, usually a local non-profit, to actually build some kind of technology to improve their organization, which is pretty cool.

After graduation, most IS majors end up working as technology analysts or consultants. A few become software engineers, though IS probably isn't the most efficient path to this (see Jack's answer). Here is last year's graduation data for IS.

In my opinion, the greatest strength of the IS program is the flexibility that it affords its students. For some, this is an incredible opportunity. It allows them to tailor their education precisely to their interests by selecting courses from around the university and truly challenging themselves in a way that no other program at CMU can.

Unfortunately, the program's greatest strength can for some students be its greatest weakness. The program has such little structure and few requirements that some students can graduate without ever challenging themselves or finding their real passion. This leads to huge differences in the abilities of students in the program.


The CS program at CMU has a completely different focus. Rather than studying the application of technology, you'll study the technology itself.

You'll study computation at a theoretical, mathematical, and implementation level. This will include studying algorithms, data structures, compilers, memory management, and operating systems (plus a lot of math).

Jonathan Paulson gave a good description of what makes the CS program unique in a different post...

What I hope sets us apart is the difficulty of the homework. Some homeworks (these are not going above-and-beyond; they are required parts of each class):
1) Wrote a compiler for most of C
2) Wrote a compiler for most of SML
3) Wrote an OS (handling hardware input, concurrency primitives, scheduling, virtual memory management, etc.)
4) Implemented MapReduce
5) Implemented malloc() and free()

There was also a lot of math + theory. Those were smaller problems, so it's harder to name something obviously impressive. Here are some required parts of the curriculum:
1) Spent 30h/week doing math problems for 15-251
2) Took another semester of algorithms *after* all the basics (basics = trees, hashtables, sorting, dynamic programming, etc.)

After graduation, almost all CS majors end up working as software engineers or go to grad school. Here is last year's graduation data for CS.


As others have said, either program is ultimately what you make of it. However, if you're interested in understanding computation with incredible breadth and depth, study CS.

If you're highly motivated and interested in studying the applications of technology to organizations while largely defining your own education, study IS.
Answer:
I am majoring in information systems with an additional major in computer science so I will offer you my perspective on this.

The two programs are separated because they have many historical, philosophical, organizational, cultural, and other differences between them. To answer your primary question, I’m just going to focus on what I see as the general structural differences.

As a disclaimer, this answer is pretty opinionated and I know some will disagree with it so please also consider other peoples' perspectives on the matter as well.


Information Systems:



Information systems is like a box of assorted chocolates. You get one little bite of everything: design, programming, business, humanities classes; it’s all in there. Will you become a great programmer, designer or whatever? Maybe, but honestly probably not by taking IS classes. Some subjects will be covered for a whole semester, while others you might spend a few weeks covering in one course your sophomore year and that’s it. The intention is that you will become familiar with each of these skills, not that you will become an expert. You might like some of the subjects and that’s great for you, but you also have to put up with all of the other subjects that you don’t like as well. I often hear students who love the business oriented courses griping about having to “actually program” something and the students who like to program complain about having to sit through business courses which they see as completely unrelated to their personal and academic interests.

To give you an example of not going into depth in a subject, consider programming. You will learn to write simple Rails apps; you will not learn how Rails works, how HTTP servers work, how relational databases work, how scripting languages like Ruby work, etc. If you don’t care about these things than that's fine for you. If you are curious, you’ll have to go elsewhere to understand them. There are similar examples for the design, business, management, and other IS focuses as well.

On the plus side, this means that you will have a decent amount of time to take many classes that are maybe only vaguely related to your major and still graduate on time.

If you would like to do some academic exploration but only be sort of proficient at lots of things, I would go with Information Systems. To briefly address your second question about information security; I would say that only being sort of proficient at security is a very bad thing for you and and whatever information you are trying to secure.


Computer Science:



SCS, on the other hand, is like going to the store and buying a couple of one pound chocolate bars and then taking them home to eat. No matter how you look at it, it’s more effort for you and a bit less diversity in what you get, but you will get a lot more in-depth knowledge in the fields you choose to study. Also note that there isn’t necessarily less to choose from, it’s just that you will have to choose fewer things.

You can become very proficient at systems programming (pretty necessary for information security), algorithmic design, machine learning, natural language processing, etc. You can even become highly proficient at a few of these, but for most people it will take a lot of effort to take on several of these at once because of the rigor of the classes you will have to take.

In SCS you must have a minor or an additional major to graduate. You may pick almost anything you like at CMU, but again, you have to get a whole degree in that subject so you will go into a fair amount of depth in that field. All of this means that you will have less time than Information Systems majors to take classes unrelated to your major or minor. You will be able to take a few classes here and there but count on being busy most semesters.


Conclusion:

If you want to go to college and sample many of the fascinating subjects that the school has to offer you, IS might be the place for you. If you want to learn a handful of subjects inside and out, I would look into SCS. The majors are very different so if you have the opportunity to choose between them, I would choose carefully.
Answer:
Other folks have touched on the primary differences between the programs, but I have a somewhat unusual story in that I graduated from the IS program, but ended up doing more CS-ish stuff in my career.

I started at CMU in 1995 as an ECE major. For financial reasons, I had to take some time off, got out of sync with the course progression in ECE, and it was going to take an extra year for me to complete the program. At that point, I was self-paying for college, so an extra year was simply unacceptable to me. I tried to transfer to SCS twice, but as others have noted, switching into SCS is notoriously difficult. The IS program (then Information & Decision Systems) was far more eager to work with me to facilitate an on-time graduation, so that's what I did.

Since college, roughly in order, I had a job in consulting, did software development for a handful of start-ups/small businesses, spent some time working at CMU in applied research, then moved on to do development for a giant multi-national science corp, then moved into management there, and most recently I've been a software engineer at Apple.

I enjoyed a lot of the things I got from the IS program. The faculty in the department were great to work with. The organizational behavior and business stuff was neat. The work we did with a local non-profit in the capstone course was rewarding. I got to take a wide variety of electives, including a handful of courses in SCS (but I recall having to fight and beg to get into them as a non-major.) I had a lot of other things going on at the time (like working multiple jobs to pay for school), so I ultimately appreciated that I had more "free" time than I might have had in SCS. Transferring from ECE to IS worked out for me in the end, and was probably the best (most practical) thing for me to do at the time.

Unfortunately, as someone who actually wanted to be an engineer all along, (initially hardware, then ultimately software) and work on some hard (i.e. fun) technical problems, this meant that there was a lot of stuff that I still had to learn, on my own, after college. I was ultimately able to learn that stuff, but it took years of doing self-guided deep dives (in my spare time), and lots and lots of reading and hands-on experimentation. It would have been far quicker to simply take OS, Distributed Systems, and Networks instead of filling in those blanks over the next 7-10 years. I also missed out on some of the math that would have made various challenges I've faced on the job over the years considerably easier (instead of learning as I went.)

As others have suggested, if you are admitted to SCS and you are actually interested in studying computer science (i.e. applied math), I would recommend you give the SCS program your first, best effort. If you want to transfer out, you'll find that a lot easier than transferring in.

To your specific question:

Can I still take classes in systems/information security if I get into the School of Computer Science?

My belief is that you can, but that you won't. You'll be busy enough as it is. I would expect the technical material in SCS to be a superset of the technical material in the IS program in every way. The stuff you won't get in SCS will be the people- and business-related material. If you really have a passion for that, and feel like you're missing out, finish your CS degree and then go get an MBA.


Question:

What is it like to attend Carnegie Mellon as an undergrad?
Answer:
On Pittsburgh. I was not an undergrad at CMU, but I have lived in Pittsburgh for 10 years now, and I will say one thing: the city has gotten significantly better in the time I've been here. The number of good restaurants, coffee shops, clubs, etc. has more than doubled.

Many of the people answering this question graduated CMU a while ago, so I think the Pittsburgh they experienced is not the Pittsburgh of today.

On hard work. The other people answering are pansies. Pain is good.
Answer:
This is written from my point of view as someone who studied in a technical department from 1997 to 2001. There is a separate College of Fine Arts that is almost totally separate socially and academically (although you can take classes there and vice versa) from the "rest" of the university.

Basically:
The weather is bad
The work is very challenging
The student body is, overall, rather nerdy
The surrounding city is of limited interest

Weather: In the winter, it's your regular snowy/slushy East Coast city. In the summer, it's muggy - so muggy that even after it finally rains, it stays humid. In the spring and summer there are about two weeks of "good" weather, and that's when they take all the sunny, idyllic pictures you see in the recruitment brochures. If you come to CMU as undergrad, be prepared for four years of unpleasant weather. When I first came out to the Bay Area and stood on Stanford's campus waiting for my ride to pick me up, I immediately and instantaneously regretted not having applied to Stanford for my undergrad, since I would have been able to get a comparable CS education without having had to suffer through the same awful weather.

Work: The academic programs are very challenging. This is good if you want to learn a lot, but not that great if you want to have a good time in college. All through college I thought I was kind of a slacker (e.g. too much time either playing video games or trying to hook up with girls), but it turns out that I did more and harder work than most people do in their undergrad years. In fact, it wasn't until years after I graduated that I found out that college is generally thought of as a carefree party time, interspersed with occasional studying - it was not at all like that for me: it was mostly work, and any time you weren't doing work was considered "slacking off" (i.e. you could be doing some work), hence my skewed self-image of being a slacker.

Nerdy: People are pretty nerdy at this school, even in the humanities departments, compared to other college campuses and the world in general. The culture of the computer science and technical departments pervades the entire campus. Again, I thought this was "normal" until I got out of it for a few years, even compared to how nerdy the South Bay of Silicon Valley Bay Area is, with a similarly poor male-female ratio. The only campus I've heard of that seems more nerdy than CMU is Caltech. The students in CFA (College of Fine Arts) are much hotter and much less nerdy than the rest of the student population, but they tend to be a distinct social group from the other departments.

Pittsburgh: Pittsburgh is one of those old and dying American cities whose heyday was sometime in the last century. It's never quite recovered from the effects of the economic downturn in the 80s, and attempts to build robotics factories in old steel mills and revitalize the waterfront have met with only limited success (EDIT 2014: the attempt revitalize the waterfront turned out to be entirely successful). Basically, the population is heavily dominated by retirees, and for the most part young people move away to other areas. This results in a steadfastly senior-citizens demographic and the city reflects that. If you like old cathedrals and gothic architecture, you might enjoy some of the city's attractions, but if you are trying to be a hip young person who is into anything other than football, Pittsburgh is not a good place to be (c.f. http://www.forbes.com/2002/06/06...). Since football doesn't usually co-interest with, say, nerdy technical types (except Charlie Cheever), you're unlikely to find much of interest in the surrounding area.


Overall, the combination of CMU, Pittsburgh, and the terrible weather make it ideal for a monk-like college undergrad experience where you will be presented with very few distractions to your extremely difficult studies, resulting in your ability to concentrate fully on them. If you are a Tiger Mom, CMU is an ideal place to send your kid because they are unlikely to find anyone to date, unlikely to find anything of interest in the surrounding city, and will most likely to spend all of their time studying or working in some research professor's lab. If you are that kind of Asian parent, you should heavily encourage your child to go to CMU.
Answer:
Disclaimer: I will say from the start that I was heavily involved with extra curriculars - went Greek for most of college, was in Math Club, Women@SCS, STOC, AB... senior year, I ran SCS Day and created regular CS drinking nights at PHI. I'm the type of person who likes to stay busy outside of class, so take that into consideration (that said, I had plenty of friends who had awesome social lives who weren't involved in anything, so take THAT into consideration as well... this is a really long disclaimer.)

I agree with the basics of Yishan's answer, so I won't touch those. The weather will suck, you will spend a LOT of time doing fun, but challenging work, everyone's a nerd in their own respects, and you will rarely leave the CMU/Squirrel Hill/UPitt area (most students don't have cars and getting elsewhere is kind of a hassle). But I'd disagree about it being ideal for a "monk-like college undergrad experience." I'd instead say it was a nerd haven, with plenty of nerdy things to do (around campus) and nerdy people to hang out with (around campus), if you're into that sort of thing. ;)

As someone who went to a public school and was shunned for being such a "nerd" and "trying too hard" in class, CMU was, and still is, the best 4 years of my life. Most people (like 90%) don't really care about seeming cool or sophisticated; walking around you hear people talking about Pokemon, Wolverine vs. Batman... there's LAN parties, CTFWS, and puzzle hunts. When it's nice out, you'll see frisbee (*gasp*, sports? yes, sports) and cricket, and probably the juggling club doing their thing. My sorority went to see High School Musical 3 in theaters together. I spent many, many nights just hanging out with my friends playing video games. And a percentage of the Greek orgs are really nerdy too -- there were themed parties on stuff like The Matrix and Snakes on a Plane. There is a ridiculous amount of things to do if you just put yourself out there and don't care about seeming weird (or rarely leaving campus).

So, yes, if you're going to want to explore the city a lot and go to keggers (they're still there, just not really the standard), you will probably be unhappy. But if playing video and board games with friends, $1 weekly blockbuster movies, and bouncy house/water gun fight events are your kind of thing, revel in it, because there is plenty of it; and leaving CMU for the real world, where those kind of things are "uncool" and "immature" is like a smack in the face.
Answer:
On Pittsburgh

Yes, it's not NYC or Chicago or San Francisco, but there is a lot of stuff going on in Pittsburgh if you seek it out. There are good museums, lots of concerts (Star Lake amphitheater is possibly my favorite music venue on earth), etc. It's cheap. You can get around without a car. The people while not worldly are generally nice. It's diverse, there is a huge young population in the Oakland/Shadyside/Squirrel Hill area thanks to CMU and Pitt being right next to one another.

There are a bunch of amazing things to do in and around the city if you're inclined toward hiking, rock climbing, mountain biking, whitewater rafting, etc. I spent a bunch of time in western PA and West VA. In fact there are a bunch of parks inside the city limits of Pittsburgh which have better mountain biking than anything within a 100 mile radius of where I now live (Chicago).

On Academics

Yes, it's hard. It's supposed to be hard (+1 Luis). Yishan's comments about the nerdiness/work ethic at CMU vs. other places is spot on. If you want to have a fun easy going college life, go somewhere else. That said, everyone I knew at CMU was better prepared for real life than most other people I met after college. For most, real work life was a breeze in comparison and even the 'slackers' at CMU wind up doing very well after school (even many of those that don't manage to graduate).

The biggest downside for me was that the curriculum didn't afford much of an opportunity to explore topics outside of one's major. The graduation requirements for most degrees are so intensive that you just don't have a lot of room for electives. I graduated with a bit of remorse that I didn't get a chance to study things I was genuinely interested in but didn't have the time to squeeze into my schedule without overloading an already demanding course load.

On Weather

It does indeed suck for the majority of the school year. However, the summers are pretty nice. I recommend staying for a summer if you have the chance, especially if you like to do outdoorsy stuff as I mentioned earlier.
Answer:
I was in the CMU INI MSIT Mobility Program and I stayed in Pittsburgh for about a year before I shifted to the Silicon Valley campus for the second part of my program.

Education:
I have been to other campuses in the states and I have to say in CMU you're surrounded by brilliant students and professors. They will inspire you, break you and push you beyond your limits. There's cutting edge research going on in almost every part of CMU that you can get involved with and contribute to backed by the biggest names in tech - Uber, Google hell we even have work on self driving cars now.

The exams are tough, especially courses like information security which CMU has been know for will really make you earn your grade. A lot of the questions in my exams were based on things I use even now in my job as a software engineer. People pull all nighters to complete assignments and grades are everything here.

Weather:
Summers are hotter than usual, winters are colder than most places with temperatures reaching -20 degrees (C) . Everything around you will turn white, the overcast weather, cloudy skies will leave you with no sunlight for months sometimes. You may not appreciate it immediately but now that I'm in California I definitely miss the seasons.

Food:
CMU has an amazing assortment of restaurants on and off campus from Indian, Chinese, Thai, Korean, American and Italian cuisines to choose from. It's pretty affordable and it's good enough to keep you running the whole day without stepping out in the harsh winter too far from campus.
Answer:
On Pittsburgh: You know, I complained about it for the majority of my four years at CMU, but Pittsburgh is actually quite a wonderful city. It's small enough to master, but large enough to feel like there's always something new popping up. The real estate is laughably cheap for the salaries paid, and though the buses are notoriously slow (59U!), they get you everywhere you need to be...for free! Certainly, you'll see less sun here than anywhere else.

On Academics: CMU is rough. I know a lot of previous answers address the fact that our training is rigorous and that you end up with a fantastic education (you do), but what I think CMU fails to understand is that you're not attending graduate school (yet). An undergraduate education should finish off your "basic" education. Nobody expects you to take that Bachelor's degree and cure cancer with it. You'll learn the specifics of bioorganic chemistry if you decide to pursue a Master's or PhD. in it. What we really lack is a true base education. Keep in mind--I'm saying this as someone who actually is pursuing a PhD. in a science. I majored in molecular biology and am now studying immunology. 70% of the classes I attended at CMU were detailed, in-depth courses that provided very little actual background information on the subject. You were just expected to keep up. Of course, when I entered graduate school, I was up to date on the newest research and more groundbreaking discoveries in my filed much more so than anyone else, but if asked a basic question in biology I'd be hard-pressed to answer.

That being said, I have never worked quite as hard as I did at CMU--and when you're told that real life, no matter what you do, is easier, you better believe it. The single most important thing I learned there was that managing your time, putting in effort, and continually reminding yourself that you can ALWAYS do better, ALWAYS work harder is the only way to keep afloat there, and in real life. CMU doesn't sugar-coat anything, and neither does anyone else. Others that I have encountered after graduation have had a hard time managing that tidbit.

On Being a Non-SCS Major: As others have mentioned, you're nobody if you're not a CS major. Where we have to use beat-up archaic lab equipment, the CS kids get upgraded facilities yearly. Get used to continually feeling like they're only keeping you around because they have to call themselves a University. I believe it's even worse if you're in the Humanities. That's not to say that we don't have phenomenal professors--we do. It's just that they have to work ten times as hard to get the same recognition as the CS guys do.

Oh and the CS graduates' salaries? Don't even ask. You won't be making that kind of money at any point in your career.

Pro-Tips:

1. Study abroad. You'll have everyone tell you that it's impossible, too hard, doesn't fit into your major. Don't listen. Do it.

2. Take classes that are not required for your major just because. No, they certainly won't be easy. Even Underwater Basketweaving at CMU probably requires knowledge of calculus. We have a bunch of really amazing people teaching classes that aren't "useful" or "technical".

3. Don't be a pre- anything major. Pre-med, pre-law... whatever. Your GPA will be way too low to pursue those fields. Don't think that you'll get a well-rounded liberal arts education here and be able to go onto law school and compete with kids who have a 4.0. Kids who are insanely smart have to work unreasonably hard and waste lots of time after graduation to try and get the "numbers" needed for law and medical school.

4. The Science and Engineering Library is the best study place on campus. Hands down. Also that back desk in the basement of Hunt is pretty awesome.

5. Luis von Ahn gets a lot of recognition, and it is well deserved. Remember though, we have a lot of other superstar professors on campus. They may not be young or cool, but they're superstars. Remind them of that. Ask them to be your mentors. Those relationships will last a lifetime, and will be the most valuable thing you take away.
Answer:
Revenge Of The Nerds
If you are a nerd-- not just a smart, hard-working student but a nerd-- who enjoys the company of other nerds, CMU is a great place to be. When I was a student there (1998-2002) our idea of a good party was having a couple of drinks and watching The Matrix on DVD. The library has a great collection of vintage science fiction paperbacks.

Workload and Focus
All of the other responses have commented on the heavy workload, and with good reason. Everyone there is working hard, and everyone wants to be seen as working hard. CMU students view high stress levels as par for the course and probably wouldn't feel right any other way, even if we sometimes flirt with burnout. I used to look forward to summer internships because I only had to work 40 hours a week and had all my evenings and weekends free, which was quite novel. There is no expectation that you'll make your coursework look effortless, nor that you will be effortlessly well-rounded with athletics and other extra-curricular activities.

People at CMU have an intensity and a sense of focus that I don't often see elsewhere in my life. Like Yishan Wong said, the notion of college as a carefree time of partying and meandering exploration doesn't really exist there, and everyone seems to be striving towards some kind of goal. If you don't have at least a nominal idea of what you want to study or do with your life this probably isn't the place for you. It's inspiring to be around that kind of work ethic and drive, and it's found in students in every single part of the university. I loved the intellectual engagement and rigor that I found there.

Gender Relations
CMU's student body was heavily male-dominated when I was there (though I understand the university has since taken steps to recruit more women), and especially so in the technical classes. Women can enjoy a large dating pool, but must put up with seeing periodic university discussion board posts about how there are no attractive women at CMU, written by men who have limited conversation skills and a poor grasp of good personal hygeine.

Brand Recognition
CMU does not necessarily have the same level of brand recognition as other top-tier universities. It enjoys an excellent reputation in the tech community, but isn't necessarily so well-known outside. "Carnegie Mellon... is that melon like the fruit? Where is that, anyway?"

Steel City
Pittsburgh isn't a premier city, and doesn't really pretend to be. As someone who grew up in the Silicon Valley, which has a mostly suburban/small town feeling and not much sense of history, I really appreciated the urban density and architectural diversity that came with living in an older city. The symphony is pretty good, there are a few interesting art museums, some large and well-maintained public parks, etc. The cost of living was very favorable. That said, at least when I lived there Pittsburgh still seemed to be struggling to find its way after the demise of the industrial base, and lacked the vibrancy one might find in a city with a stronger economy and a more youthful population. I didn't encounter many people at CMU who intended to stay in Pittsburgh after finishing their studies (unless they were already local natives). However, by the time I left for good in 2004 business development was picking up so maybe things have improved.

Also, yes, the weather is really terrible.

Community
I feel a commonality with other CMU alumni that I don't have with people I've met in other contexts. Perhaps it's the shared sense of trial by fire-- see above above about the workload-- but the friendships I've kept up with people I met there feel deep and long-lasting, and can be revived even after long periods of separation.
Answer:
On Luis Von Ahn:

Boss.

On Carnegie Mellon:

Carnegie Mellon is Disney Land for the curious. They're at the center of every great technological advancement that will take place over the next few decades.

The best part is, if you want to know something-- if you want to get something done, and you're in way over your head, there's a person (or 12) within 1000 feet that can help.

The social dynamic is definitely a bit a strange-- "what's your major?" is the conversation starter of choice. But everyone is really approachable--especially when talking about shared interests.

On one hand, there's an attitude of, "well, it seems certain that a few of us should make it, so why not us?" -- on the other hand, there's this placid comfort that we should have an above average salary and an upper-middle class lifestyle at Microsoft, Google, Facebook, Goldman, Deloitte, etc.

If there is a weakness in the undergraduate student body, it's a collective-attitude of OK, just tell me what to do and I'll do it. While it succeeds in making classes more competitive (and fraternities more expansive), it also takes away from the other things you should learn in college. Like how to talk people and handle relationships. And start companies.

There's plenty of cultural diversity, which is awesome if you make the time to explore it. But what happens a lot of the time-- I think -- is that diversity finds comfort in the familiar. Small groups form. And socio-economic tunnel-vision ensues.

On the matter of women, yes its true. There's less blonde-haired blue-eyed bombshells at CMU. I'm sure the equivalent is true of the men. But Pittsburgh girls seem to like CMU boys, and CMU boys seem to love CMU girls. (Pittsburgh girls also LOVE ranch BTW) and it's a college town for chrissake, so there's no real need to worry about that.

Also, Pittsburgh is one of the greatest cities on earth. Unlike San Francisco, the fog comes from the steel factories, and everyone is pretty grounded.

And so now when it's all said and done, I can honestly say that I would not trade my experience at CMU for anything... with a net present value less than $168,234.72
Answer:
Disclaimer: I graduated from CMU in 2001, so things may have changed somewhat since I was there.

I went to CMU as a non-technical major, so my experience was quite a bit different than that of the CS majors who most people think of when they hear "CMU." For one thing, if you are a non-technical major, expect that your department will be underfunded compared to the CS department. We would see the CS department constantly showered with money and upgrades, while it was a huge deal when the English department (which I was part of) got a few chairs for a lounge. (Woo, livin' large!) There is a pervasive attitude on campus that only the technical students are working hard and that the rest of the student body is taking the easy way out. (Note: at CMU, there is no taking the easy way out.) If you're not into nerd culture, it can be pretty offputting to suddenly be surrounded by it and see things like LARPing and encounter the unfortunate tendency of some technical students to be neglectful of hygiene and think that showering is optional.

On the plus side, there is a favorable male-female ratio (well, favorable if you're a woman). The campus is fairly small, so even the large lecture classes are not actually that large if you compare them to classes at a state school. Pittsburgh has a lot of negatives, but it's a safe city and is full of pierogies and other delicious Polish and German foods. It's also not an expensive city if you decide to remain there post-graduation.

CMU is a school for working hard. If that's what you want to do, you'll find it pretty satisfying overall, regardless of your major. Though if that major isn't CS, you should also expect a good dose of the "on the outside looking in" feeling.
Answer:
When the opposing team misses a goal, the crowd shouts "It's okay, no-one scores at CMU." By "the crowd," I mean "the band."
A fraternity sells T-shirts that read "Sex kills. Come to CMU and live forever"
In a talk about Bayesian reasoning, I was asked "You see a college student who seems socially awkward. Is he more likely to be a math or business major?" I thought "Hmmm....business majors are usually pretty socially awkward..."
You might think that Game Design or Calligraphy would be fun classes to balance out a tough workload. Both classes can take 40+ hours per week.
I saw a Red Bull ad in the campus convenience store that read "No-one wishes they slept more in college." I stopped drinking Red Bull in protest.
If your user ID is remotely pronounceable, people will call you by it (at least in CS). Or even if it's not. Someone might walk up to me and say "Hey jkoppel! I just heard from pcd that...."


Question:

Who are your favorite CMU professors? Why?
I'd especially like to hear about professors that are dynamic speakers
Answer:
John Mackey, Robert Harper, and Luis Von Ahn are great speakers and even better teachers.
Answer:
I am amazed almost no one has listed David Eckhardt yet. He only teaches one class, Operating Systems, but he pours his heart into it. His lectures are well-honed, funny, and very instructive. He discusses a broad range of topics, some explicitly relevant to the projects being worked on, and some just there because they're interesting or fun to think about. He provides excellent feedback when he reads and marks up your code (and he does! Every line you write gets read!), and he writes excellent exams (along with the TAs, of course).
In addition to being an excellent professor, he's a great boss. Staff meetings are fun, and Dave is a really funny and nice guy when you get to know him. I worked for him for 3 semesters, and I have absolutely no regrets. That class would not be the same without him, and I would argue it's the best class in all of CS at CMU.

I also want to mention Thomas Keating, who teaches Technical Communication for Computer Scientists. He gets a bad rap because his class is decidedly unpopular, but in smaller class sizes, he is really a great teacher. I took tech comm over the summer, and I actually really enjoyed it. I learned a lot. He's a funny guy, and I really wish he got the credit he deserved.

In physics, Kunal Ghosh and Robert Swendsen teach (among other things) matter & interactions 2, the advanced E&M intro class. They're good lecturers, and when I walked into the final exam an hour and a half late (oops!) they offered to stay with me to give me the full three hours. I ended up not needing it, but the fact that they offered was really great of them.

Also worth noting: Klaus Sutner is a very funny and personable guy, who gives great lectures on subjects that interest him. John Mackey is, as almost everyone has said, an amazing lecturer and a stand-up guy.
Answer:
In my opinon, David Kosbie. His introduction to programming class is perhaps the most inspiring class I've ever taken. I loved hearing his life lessons and pep-talks about work ethics, and I really appreciated his high expectations of himself, his staff and his students. He also created a lot of fun in the class, and gave the students a lot of room for creativity in his homeworks. Simply an excellent teacher!
Answer:
Some professors that haven’t already been mentioned:

Manuel Blum (Algorithms) - His class was a lot of fun, and I loved how he injected interesting personal anecdotes into his teaching. Bonus - He distributes candy and pizza after quizzes.

David O'Halloran (Introduction to Computer Systems) - A very dedicated teacher, and his passion for teaching shows.

Roni Rosenfeld (Machine Learning) - A great instructor. He encouraged classroom participation and made the class really interesting with his discussions.

Carolyn Rose (Applied Machine Learning) - Energetic and enthusiastic professor. Super nice too.

Rajeev Gandhi (Fundamentals of Embedded Systems) - I didn't take his class, but I have heard from multiple people that he is a superb instructor.

Answer:
Hyong Kim - Teaches 18-756: Packet Switching and Computer Networks every Fall and 18-757: Network Management and Control every other spring semester.

I have an obvious bias towards him (given that he was my adviser plus I TA'ed 18-756). He is one of the very few professors who has had successful industry/startup experience along with academic background. Detail-oriented with occasional humor. Additional brownie points for being the only networking course in the university which has the right blend of telecom (ATM, SONET/SDH, MPLS) + datacom along with switch architecture design.
